Executive Summary                        
 
E.1 Overview 
Theexecutivesummaryprovidesthekeyfindingsfromtheexistingconditionsanalysisforthe
ShorelineCommunityTransportationStudy.

LocatedwithintheCityofMountainView,thestudyareaforthisprojectistheNorthBayshoreChange
Areaasdefinedinthe2030CityofMountainViewGeneralPlanandwhichwillbefurtherstudiedin
theNorthBayshorePrecisePlanupdate.ThestudyareaisboundedbyShorelineParkandtheSan
FranciscoBaytothenorth,AdobeCreekandthePaloAltoBaylandsParktothewest,andStevens
Creektotheeast.Highway101boundsthesouthernandwesternsidesofthestudyarea.

ItisimportanttonotethatthestudyeffortswillextendbeyondtheboundariesoftheNorthBayshore
ChangeAreainordertohelpdefineandimprovetheexistingandfuturetransportationconnections
betweenNorthBayshoreandthecoreeconomicandemploymentcentersinMountainView,including
theMountainViewTransportationCenterinDowntownandtheNASAAmesresearcharea.

StakeholderoutreachfortheprojecthasincludedmeetingswithseveralShorelineareaemployersand
interestgroups,Caltrainplatformandshuttlesurveysaswellasonestakeholderworkshop.

E.2 Travel Behavior 
AccordingtotheinformationprovidedbyGoogleandLinkedIn(twoofthelargestemployersinthe
Shorelinestudyarea)(seeTablesE1andE2,andFiguresE1andE2),employeesfromthe
differentcompaniesaregenerallylivinginandcommutingfromsimilarlocations.Morethanhalfof
theemployeesfrombothcompanies(52.5percentfromGoogleand51.2percentfromLinkedIn)
commutefromSantaClaraCounty,whichisthecountywheretheShorelineareaislocated.
Furthermore,morethan20percentofemployeesfromeachcompany(21.7percentfromGoogleand
21.1percentfromLinkedIn)commutefromSanFranciscoCounty,whichislocatedbetween25and32
milesfromtheShorelinearea.Thegeneraltrendshownbythesenumbersisthatmostemployeeslive
closetowheretheyworkandasthedistancefromtheofficelocationincreases,thenumberof
employeeslivingincertaincountiesdecreasesdramatically.ThisissummarizedinaGISanalysis
providedbyIntuit,whichshowedthatmostemployeeslivedclosetotheIntuitofficelocationandthe
numberofemployeesinothercountiesdroppedasthedistancetotheirofficeincreased.However,
thehighpercentageofemployeeslivinginSanFranciscoiscontrarytothistrend.

FiguresE1andE2werecreatedtobetterunderstandandshowtherelationshipbetweenthe
distancefromtheworker'shometoShorelineandthenumberofworkerslivingincertaincounties.
TheworkerpopulationfiguresbycountyprovidedbyGoogleandLinkedInweredividedintospecific
citieswithineachcountyusingthepopulationdistributionshownthroughthe2010U.S.Census
figures.

                                                                                  Executive Summary 
 

ThemajorityoftraveldemandwithintheShorelinestudyareaisgeneratedfromemploymentbased
trips.ThefourmajoremployerswithinthestudyareaareGoogle,Intuit,Microsoft,andLinkedIn.
Currentlytheseemployersutilizetransportationdemandmanagement(TDM)measurestoencourage
alternativetravelmodes,recognizingtheinherentdifficultyofaccessingtheareaviasingleoccupancy
vehicles(SOV)duetothegeographicalconstraintsoftheShorelineareawhichlimittrafficcapacity
andaccesstotheregionaltransitnetwork.Inaddition,someemployersareactivelyseekingtoreduce
emissionsbyincreasingtheiremployees'alternativemodeaccess.TableE3summarizesthecurrent
TDMmeasuresemployedbyGoogle,Microsoft,LinkedIn,andIntuit.

Basedoninterviewswithmajoremployersinthearea,thesuccessofnonSOVtraveloptionstendsto
becorrelatedwiththedistanceanemployeelivesfromtheworkplace.AccordingtobothGoogleand
Intuit,therewashighregionaltransitandshuttleuseforemployeeswholivemorethan10milesfrom
theoffice,duetotheprovisionsofTDMmeasuresthatallowpeakperiodcommutetripstoandfrom
worktobereasonablealternativestodrivingtothestudyarea.However,employeeswholiveless
than10milesawaytendtobemorereluctanttochoosealternativemodeswhencommutingtothe
Shorelinearea,astheircommutebyautomobileisreasonablyconvenientwhencomparedtothe
availabilityofhighqualitylocaltransitserviceandbicycle/pedestrianinfrastructure.

E.3 Existing Bicycle and Pedestrian Conditions 
TheShorelineareahasadevelopedbutsomewhatincompletebicycleandpedestriannetwork.
Despitetheamountofexistingbicycleinfrastructureinthestudyarea,gapsexistbetweenoriginsand
destinations,bothwithinthestudyareaandbeyond.Additionally,thecurrentbicycleinfrastructureis
primarilygearedtowardsthe"confidentandenthusiastic"bicyclistandhaselementsthatmaybe
dauntingto"interestedbutconcerned"bicyclists.InfrastructureincludesseveralClassIbicycleonly
pathsandClassIIstripedbicyclelanestraversinginandthroughthestudyarea.

Pedestrianinfrastructurewithinthestudyareaiswelldeveloped,withmanysidewalks,crosswalks,
andtrails.Thephysicallayout(longblocks)ofsomeroadwaysareanimpedimenttogoodpedestrian
connectivityandaccessibility.Furthermore,roadwayinfrastructuresegregatesdifferenttravel

E.4 Existing Traffic Conditions 
Currently,trafficintoandoutofthestudyareaisconstrainedbythelimitednumberofaccesspoints.
AsTableE9andTableE10shows,mostgatewayroadwaysintotheShorelineareaalready
experiencehighvolume,whileroadwayvolumesareatorapproachingcapacityatseverallocations.
Ofalltheaccesspoints,ShorelineBoulevardservesthehighestvolumeoftraffic,mostlylikelyduetoit
beingtheeasternmostaccesspointforpeoplecomingfromtheSouthBay,aswellasitsstrategic
locationadjacenttothehighestdensityofemploymentusesandasthemainaccessfortheShoreline
Amphitheatre.

Ofthefourgatewaylocations,onlyShorelineBoulevardexceedsexistingroadwaycapacityduringthe
highestdemandtravelperiods.However,theremainingthreeroadwayaccesspointsarebelowfull

WhiletransportationaccessandalternativesfortheNorthBayshoreemploymentareaare

           NewAuxiliaryandHOVLanes(andfutureExpressLanes)on101willincreasefreewaycapacity
           byabout1520percentandprovidebettertraveltimeforexpressbusesandridesharing.

           Newtransitservicesarebeingdevelopedthatcansignificantlyincreasethepotentialnumberof
           transitusers,including:

           -   TheplannedBARTExtension(WarmSpringsandMilpitas/Berryessa)andpotentialVTA
               LRTsystemimprovementswillenhanceconnectionstothesouthandeast.

           -   TheCaltrainElectrificationprogramwillimproveservicequalityandcapacity.

           -   BusRapidTransit(BRT)servicesareplannedforElCaminoRealbyVTAandSamTrans.

           -   TheDumbartonExpressbusserviceisconsideringanewroutefromtheEastBaytoNorth
               Bayshore

           Bicyclecommutinghasbeenincreasingsignificantlyinthepast10yearsand,withimproved
           facilitiesandaccesspoints,canplayanimportantroleforshortercommutetrips.

           Technologyadvancessuchasautonomousvehicles,personalrapidtransit(PRT),andintelligent
           transportationsystems,promiseimprovedtransportationsystemefficienciesforalltypesof
           travelwhichwillallowcapacityincreaseswithoutsignificantnewfacilityconstruction.

           ThenewGeneralPlancreatesincentivesandrequirementsforemployersanddevelopersthat
           willmotivatethemtoaddressthecommutingneedsoftheirexistingandfutureemployees.

           Onsiteservices,suchasdaycare,cafes,drycleaning,andthelikeallowemployeestotakecare
           oferrandsthatwouldotherwiserequireacar,allowingemployeestoreduceoreliminate
           unnecessarytrips.

           Improvedcommunicationstoolsandothertechnologieswilllikelyreducetheneedfortravel
           overtimeasmoreemployeesworkathomeoratsatellitelocationsclosertohomeandtheneed
           totravelformeetingsandconferencesisreduced.

Section 1    
Introduction 
1.1 Plan Purpose 
TheShorelineRegionalParkCommunity/NorthBayshoreAreaoftheCityofMountainView
(ShorelineCommunity)facesafundamentaldilemmaoftransportationandlanduse.Thisareais
clearlyanareaofopportunityintheCityandisexpectedtobethefocusoflongtermgrowthwith
expansionanddevelopmentofthehightechnologycampusesandothermixeduselanduses.Itisalso
clearthatthecurrenttransportationinfrastructureisnotsufficienttosupportthistypeofgrowth,and
thatasignificantexpansionoftheroadwaynetworkisnotpracticalordesirable.Thesolutiontothis
dilemmawillbetheimplementationoftransportationmeasuresandstrategiesdesignedtoreduce
relianceonthesingleoccupancyvehicle(SOV),encourageusesofalternativetravelmodes,and
managethetotaldemandfortravel.ThepurposeoftheShorelineCommunityTransportationStudy
willbetodevelopthistransportationstrategythroughacollaborativeprocessthatincludesthekey
stakeholdersandcommunityinterests.

1.2 Report Overview 
TheExistingConditionsreportisorganizedintothefollowingmainsections:

       Introduction,PlanPurposeandRelatedPlanningEfforts

       TravelBehavior

       ExistingBicycleandPedestrianConditions

       ExistingTrafficConditions

       ExistingTransitOperations

1.2 Study Area 
TheStudyareaistheNorthBayshoreareaasdefinedintheGeneralPlan2030andtheNorth
BayshorePrecisePlanandisillustratedinFigure11onthefollowingpage.TheNorthBayshore
studyareaisconstrainedgeographicallyandphysicallybynaturalandmanmadeboundaries
surroundingit.Inparticular,ShorelineParkandtheSanFranciscoBayliestothenorth,AdobeCreek
andthePaloAltoBaylandsParkboundthewesternside,whileStevensCreekmakesupaphysical
boundaryofthestudyareaontheeast.USHighway101boundsthesouthernandwesternsidesofthe
Shorelinearea.

ItisimportanttonotethatthestudyeffortswillextendbeyondtheboundariesoftheNorthBayshore
Areainordertohelpdefineandimprovetheexistingandfuturetransportationconnectionsbetween
NorthBayshoreandthecoreeconomicandemploymentcentersinMountainView(theMountain
ViewTransportationCenterinDowntownandtheNASAAmesresearcharea).

1.4 Related Planning Efforts 
1.4.1 General Plan 
The2030MountainViewGeneralPlanprojectstheNorthBayshorearea,designateda"changearea"
toincreaseitsemploymentdensityoverthenexttwentyyears.Nonewhousingisenvisionedwithin
theNorthBayshorearea.TheplancallsforShorelineBoulevardtobereimaginedasthemainspineof
thearea,withgreaterintensitiesandwithafocusonstreetfrontinggroundfloorretail.Theplancalls
formoreandbetterbicyclistandpedestrianconnectionsthroughtheexistinglargeblockformatof
thestreetnetwork.

Themobilityelementofthegeneralplanconfersthe"Avenue"typologytoRengstorffAvenue,
CharlestonRoad,AmphitheatreParkway,andShorelineBoulevardfromCharlestonRoadto
CrittendenLane.ShorelineBoulevardfromHighway101toCharlestonRoadisdesignatedas"Major
RetailStreet"typology,andallotherstreetsaredesignatedas"FlexibleStreet"typology.

The"Avenue"typologypresumestheroleofdistributingtripstocommercialandresidentialareas.All
modesoftransportationaregivenequalweightonthe"Avenue"typology,withbicyclistsgetting
priorityalongestablishedbicyclecorridors.

The"MajorRetailStreet"typologyisuniquetothissectionofShorelineBoulevard,andismeantto
distributeregionalvehicletripsgeneratedfromHighway101whilestillprovidinghighquality
amenitiesforbicyclists,pedestrians,andtransit.

The"FlexibleStreet"typologyismeantfortransitionalareaswithprimarilylocallyservingtraffic.
Travelspeedsshouldbereducedonthesestreetstoprovideparitybetweenmodesoftransportation,
withbicyclistsandpedestriansgivenhighpriority.

1.4.2 North Bayshore Precise Plan 
The2030GeneralPlanisthefoundationfortheNorthBayshorePrecisePlan.Currentelementsofthe
NorthBayshorePrecisePlaninclude:

           Aprojectwebsiteforpublicparticipation(http://www.northbayshorepreciseplan.org/)
           TheNorthBayshoreopenforumincludingareastakeholders(employees,residents,property
           owners)thatmeettoreviewkeydeliverablesanddiscussideasforimplementingkeypolicy
           directions;
           DetailedTrafficStudyoftheNorthBayshoretravelshedincludingselectlinks,critical
           intersectionsandfreewaysegmentsandramps.
           ActivecoordinationwiththeShorelineCommunityTransportationStudyinthedevelopment
           andevaluationofalternativeconcepts.

andpedestrians,oneofwhich(PermanenteCreek)connectsdirectlytothebikelaneswithinthestudy

thecurrentbicycleinfrastructureisprimarilygearedtowardsthe"confidentandenthusiastic"
bicyclistandhaselementsthataredauntingto"interestedbutconcerned"bicyclists.

ThepedestriansidewalknetworkintheNorthBayshorestudyareavariessignificantly.Astechnology
companiesredeveloptheirproperties,sidewalkswereoftenenhancedtothestandardsinvogueatthe

oldestareas,hasnarrowattachedsidewalksthataresometimesincomplete.Inthecentralareaof

Duetothedevelopmentpatternofmanytechcampusesoverthelastfewdecades,mostofthe

setbackbuildingsandprohibitedonstreetparkingprovidesverylittleutilityandactivityonmany
sidewalksintheplanarea.Manysidewalkswithintheplanareaarethesoledomainofthepleasure

3.3 Pedestrian and Bicycle Issues 
Majorbicycletrailandinfrastructureissuesincludelimitedtrailconnections,whichresultsinpartial
recreationalandcommuteraccessavailabilityforemployeesandvisitorstotheShorelinearea.For
instance,adhoctrailconnectionsarepresentalongsectionsofCharlestonRoadonStevensCreek
Trail,whilenotrailaccessisprovidedtothewestsideofPermanenteCreek.Inaddition,physical
barrierspreventbicycleandpedestriansfromaccessingtheShorelineareafromexternallocales
withoutexperiencingsomedifficulty,resultinginadetrimentalimpactforbicyclistsandpedestrians
seekingtotraveltoShoreline.Locationsincludethelackofmediananddedicatedbicyclecrossingon
CharlestonRoadforPermanenteCreekTrail,andtheinclusionofafencebetweenStevensCreekTrail
andtheNASAAmestrails,whichimpedetravelbetweenthetwoareas.

Alsonotedissuesincludedgapsinthenetworkdonotallowbicycliststoconvenientlyandquickly
maketripstoandthroughthestudyarea,resultinginmoredifficulttravelthroughthestudyarea.
ExamplesincludethePermanenteCreekTrail,whichendsbeforereachingtheMiddlefieldRoad
bicyclelanes,aswellastheGapbetweenBayshoreParkwaybikelanesandBayTrailtrailheadon
TerminalBoulevard.Lastly,thelackofwayfindingsignagemaycausebicyclistsandpedestrians
seekingtoreachtheShorelineareatonotbeabletodososincelimitedsignageisavailabletoindicate
easiestandmostconvenientmethodsofaccessintotheShorelinearea.

Overall,whileopportunitiestobicycleandwalktotheShorelineareaareavailable,lowamountsof
tripsviathesemodesaremadeduetothedifficultyoftravelintothestudyareaasaresultofabsolute
distanceoftheareafromotherresidentialcommunities,aswellaskeybicycleandpedestrian
infrastructuredeficiencieswhichcoulddiscouragenoviceandaveragebikeridersandpedestrians
fromtraversingacrossUS101alonggatewayroadwaysorseparatefacilitiesduetothechallengesof
accessingandusingthefacilities.

4.1.1 Regional Roadways 
UnitedStatesHighway101(US101)providesnorthsouthregionalaccesstotheNorth
Bayshore/ShorelineareaalongthePeninsulaportionoftheSanFranciscoBayArea.US101servesas
theprimaryregionalconnectorfortheCityofMountainViewtotherestofthePeninsulaaswellas
connectingnorthwardtoSanFranciscoandMarinCountyandsouthwardtotheSouthBay.Inthe
studyarea,US101hasfourlanesineachdirection,operatinginanorthwestsoutheastdirection.In
bothdirections,aHighOccupancyVehicle(HOV)laneforvehicleswithtwoormoreoccupants
operatesintheleftmostlanethroughtheentirestudyarea.Itoperatesduringthemorningpeak
commutebetween5A.M.and9A.M.,andduringtheeveningpeakcommutebetween3P.M.and7P.M.
AccesstothestudyareafromUS101areprovidedbythreeinterchanges,atSanAntonioRoad,
RengstorffAvenue,andShorelineBoulevard,fromnorthtosouth,respectively.

StateRoute85providesnorthsouthregionalaccesstothesouthernendoftheShorelineareafrom
connectingMountainViewandSouthSanJose.Highway85isanimportantnorthsouthconnection
betweenUS101andInterstate280.ItintersectswithStevensCreekTrailandUS101inMountain
ViewatMoffettFieldatthesouthernendoftheShorelinestudyarea.SR85hasHOVlanesthroughout
itsentirelength.AccesstothestudyareafromSR85isprovidedattheMoffettBlvdinterchange.

TheShorelinestudyareaisauniqueemployerbasedareathatisgeographicallyconstrainedby
waterwaysandUSHighway101.TheShorelineareaistwotofivemileswalking,bikingordriving
distancefromtheDowntownMountainViewTransitCenter.Therearesignificantgapsinlastmile
connectivityandmissingopportunitiestolinktotheregionalandlocalpublictransitnetworkwhich
primarilyservesdowntownMountainViewandothercitycentersalongtheSanFranciscoPeninsula.
Asaresult,apatchworkprivatetransitsystemhasbeendevelopedtoservethestudyarea,primarily
madeupofemployerpaidshuttlesforemployeesofthelargeemployercampusestocommutetoand
fromtheShorelinearea.Theseshuttleservicesaremainlyofferedviaregionalcoachstylebusesand
localvanpoolandshuttles,whichprovidedirectservicefromtransitconnectionsandneighborhoods
inareasasfarawayasSanFranciscoandtheEastBay,aswellastoandfromtheDowntownMountain
ViewCaltrainStation.

operatorsdoprovidelocalpublictransitservicetotheShorelinestudyarea.TheseincludeCaltrain,

5.2 Summary of Shoreline Transit Service 
Peopleutilizingtransittoaccessthestudyareaarefacedwithanadhocandnonintegrated
transportationsystemthatisprimarilygearedtoserveemployeestravelingduringthepeakcommute
periods.Forpeopletravelingviaregionaltransit,allrailservicesonlyassistShorelinebound
passengerstogetnearthestudyarea;ridersmuststillfindanothermodeoftransportationtogetinto
theShorelinearea.Localridersalsosufferfrominfrequenttransitservice.Thisoverallgapinpublic
transitserviceforcesriderstoseeklocalpublicorprivatetransittocompletetheirtrip.To
supplementandserveasthe"firstmile,lastmile"solution,Shorelineemployerssupportprivateand
Caltrainshuttlestoconnectfromregionalrailconnectionsandlocaltransithubs.Theseshuttlesplay
animportantroleduringthemorningandeveningcommutes,connectingemployeeswiththeirfinal
destinationorprovidingtheinitialtransfertopublictransit.Withouttheseshuttles,employeeswould
haveveryfewconvenientoptionstogetcommuteintotheShorelinearea.

Thepatchworkoftransitservicebetweenpublicandprivatetransitisadirectresultofthesizable
increaseinemploymentinthepastdecade,insufficienttransitplanningtoaccommodatethis
employmentincrease,andtheresultantlackoffrequentandaccessiblelocaltransitserviceintothe
studyarea.Inaddition,employersalsofelttheneedtoprovidealternativemodesofaccesstothe
studyareainsteadofbyprivatevehicle,inordertoallowemployerstocompetewithother
employmentclusters/officeparksforemployeesconcernedabout"greener"alternativestodrivingor
easeoftransitaccesstothearea.Overall,thisresultsinatransitsystemwhereprivateemployers
essentiallyreplacelocaltransitwiththeirownprivateshuttlestoallowemployeestoreachtheir
campuses.

Ultimately,thelackoftransitintegrationbetweenexistingpublictransitprovidersandprivate
employerbasedshuttleshasresultedinadearthofconvenientlocaltransit.Localresidentsand
commutersarethusprovidedonlywithlimitedamountsofdirectlocalpublictransitserviceintothe
Shorelinearea.TheVTAbusservicesthatarebetterabletoservetheShorelineareaareonlyan
optionforthosecommuterslivingandworkingclosetotheexistinglines.However,thelackof
frequencyandconveniencetolargeresidentialordenseneighborhoodsresultsinmanylocal
commuterstousetheirprivatevehicletocommute.Thiscorrelateswithemployerbasedsurveys
whichindicatethatshorttripstoandfromtheShorelineareabyemployeesaremadeprimarilyby
personalvehicle.

Appendix A
Rider Intercept Survey
                                  Mountain View Shoreline Transportation Study:

                                               Rider Intercept Survey


Instructions

This survey is intended to capture the travel habits of people commuting to and from the Shoreline area in Mountain
View. The goal of this survey is to capture approximately 100-150 unique responses from individuals boarding Caltrain
during the afternoon commute period. Surveyors will approach pedestrians on the platform at Downtown Mountain
View Caltrain Station and ask them if they would be willing to answer a brief, 1-2 minute survey about commuting in the
area. Questions are written in a way that is intended to elicit simple responses with minimal need for interpretation on
the part of the surveyor.

Survey Times:

Surveying will occur across one or two 2- to 3-hour windows

         Thursday (7/26) from 4:30pm-6:30pm
         Follow up date (7/31) Tuesday from 4:30pm-6:30pm


Five (5) surveyors will be working during each window. In order to achieve the desired overall response rate of 100-150
responses, each surveyor should aim to obtain a minimum of 20-30 responses during their shift.

Survey Locations:

Downtown Mountain View Caltrain Station
Administering the Survey:

Approaching Respondent

        Surveyors should identify themselves as conducting a study for the City of Mountain View.
        Ask pedestrians if they would be willing to answer a brief, 1-2 minute survey about their travel/commute
        experience.
        If pedestrian declines to participate, thank them and record a non-response.
        If pedestrian agrees to participate, thank them and administer the survey.


Administering the survey

    (1) Every time a surveyor approaches a pedestrian the event should be recorded. If the pedestrian declines to
        participate the surveyor should tally a non-response for the appropriate hour and survey location.
    (2) The survey has 6 questions. If a respondent walks away or asks to stop part way through the survey, thank
        them and mark all remaining questions as "NR" for non-response. These surveys will still be tallied, analyzed,
        and will be counted as part of the 150 target responses.
    (3) Each question on the survey has multiple responses. Ideally, the surveyor will read the question and will be
        able to categorize the respondents answer without having to also read them all the available choices. Surveyors
        should only prompt the respondent with choices as needed to help the survey proceed efficiently
    (4) The surveyor can use reasonable discretion to match a respondent's answer to a category
    (5) If the respondent's answer does not match any of the categories on the sheet, the surveyor should mark
        "other" and move on

Appendix B
Shuttle Driver Survey
                                  Mountain View Shoreline Transportation Study:

                                           Shuttle Driver Intercept Survey


Instructions

This survey is intended to capture the travel habits of people commuting to and from the Shoreline area in Mountain
View. The goal of this survey is to capture approximately 20-30 unique responses from shuttle drivers during the
morning commute period. Surveyors will approach shuttle drivers parked near the platform at Downtown Mountain
View Caltrain Station and ask them if they would be willing to answer a brief, 5-7 minute survey about commuting in the
area. Questions are written in a way that is intended to elicit simple responses with minimal need for interpretation on
the part of the surveyor.

Survey Times:

Surveying will occur across one or two 2- to 3-hour windows

         Thursday (7/12) from 7:00am-9:30am

         Follow up date (7/17) Tuesday from 7:00am-9:30am

Three (3) surveyors will be working during each window. In order to achieve the desired overall response rate of 20-30
responses, each surveyor should aim to obtain a minimum of 7-10 responses during their shift.

Survey Locations:

Downtown Mountain View Caltrain Station
Administering the Survey:

Approaching Respondent

        Surveyors should identify themselves as conducting a study for the City of Mountain View.

        Ask drivers if they would be willing to answer a brief, 5-7 minute survey about the travel/commute experience.

        If driver declines to participate, thank them and record a non-response.

        If driver agrees to participate, thank them and administer the survey.



Administering the survey

    (1) Every time a surveyor approaches a driver the event should be recorded. If the driver declines to participate the
        surveyor should tally a non-response for the appropriate hour and survey location.

    (2) The survey has 13 questions. If a respondent walks away or asks to stop part way through the survey, thank
        them and mark all remaining questions as "NR" for non-response. These surveys will still be tallied, analyzed,
        and will be counted as part of the 30 target responses.

    (3) Each question on the survey has multiple responses. Ideally, the surveyor will read the question and will be
        able to categorize the respondents answer without having to also read them all the available choices. Surveyors
        should only prompt the respondent with choices as needed to help the survey proceed efficiently

    (4) The surveyor can use reasonable discretion to match a respondent's answer to a category

    (5) If the respondent's answer does not match any of the categories on the sheet, the surveyor should mark
        "other" and move on

                                                              Thursday:            6-7 AM   7-8 AM 8-9 AM   9-10 AM

The North Bayshore study area has a fairly developed bicycle network. There are bike lanes on two of the
three overcrossings accessing the study area, as well as bike lanes on sections of Bayshore Parkway, Garcia
Avenue, Charleston Road, Amphitheatre Parkway, Rengstorff Avenue, and Shoreline Boulevard. There are
also two multi-use trails providing vehicle-separated access to the study area for bicyclists and pedestrians,
one of which (Permanente Creek) connects directly to the bike lanes within the study area.

Despite the amount of existing bicycle infrastructure in Mountain View, significant gaps still exist between
origins and destinations, both within and outside the study area. Additionally, the current bicycle
infrastructure is primarily geared towards the "confident and enthusiastic" bicyclist and has elements which
are daunting to "interested but concerned" potential bicyclists.

The pedestrian sidewalk network in the North Bayshore study area varies significantly. As technology
companies redevelop their properties, sidewalks were often enhanced to the standards in vogue at the time of
construction. This leaves varying sidewalk (and street) typologies depending upon the year of construction.
The northwestern area of North Bayshore, one of the oldest areas, has narrow attached sidewalks that are
sometimes incomplete. In the central area of North Bayshore, sidewalks are often detached from the street.
These sidewalks tend to meander through trees and bushes, sometimes elevated from the street by a
landscaped berm. The Shoreline Boulevard overcrossing, having been recently rebuilt, has wide curb-attached
sidewalks. A sidewalk suitability map is located in Appendix A.

Most of the buildings in the North Bayshore study area are seet back from the street, with large amounts of
surface parking separating the sidewalk and the building doors. The combination of set-back buildings and
prohibited on-street parking results in limited utility and low levels of walking on many sidewalks in the plan
area. Many sidewalks within the plan area are the sole domain of the pleasure-stroll due to the impracticality
of walking for any other purpose.




The San Antonio Road overcrossing of Highway 101 was
described in Appendix B. Many shuttle buses run by
employers in the study area use the San Antonio Road
offramp.

The existing bike lanes on E Bayshore Road cut out for
eastbound bicyclists before the San Antonio Road, where
an additional vehicle travel lane is added for turning
vehicles. E Bayshore Road, west of San Antonio Road, is in
the City of Palo Alto. Westbound on Bayshore Parkway, a
free right-turn pocket cuts through the bike lane at the
intersection. There is a small segment of bike lane at the intersection adjacent to a pedestrian island for the
crosswalk on the northern leg of the intersection.

San Antonio Road is a designated bike route north of this intersection to Terminal Boulevard. There are no
sidewalks on the western side the street, and narrow curb-attached sidewalks on the eastern side of the
street. Sections of sidewalk are missing along the east side of San Antonio Road at Casey Avenue.

San Antonio Road has a ROW of approximately 67 feet at the intersection with Bayshore Parkway, a ROW of
approximately 50 feet where an access road branches off further to the north and a ROW of approximately 31
feet north of Casey Avenue. The west side of San Antonio Road does not allow parking at any time, and the
east side of San Antonio Road allows parking north of the point at which the access road on the west side of
San Antonio Road branches off (approximately 175 feet south of Casey Avenue).




Bayshore Parkway has bike lanes from San Antonio Road in the west to Garcia Avenue in the east, a stretch of
roadway where no street parking is allowed. Bike lanes end on Bayshore Parkway east of Garcia Avenue, and
bike lanes continue on Garcia Avenue east to Amphitheatre Parkway. There is no signage on either Bayshore
Parkway or Garcia Avenue directing bicyclists where bicycle infrastructure continues.

There are no sidewalks on the western side of Bayshore
Parkway (which fronts a landscape strip between the
street and Highway 101), and there is a narrow, attached
sidewalk on the eastern side of Bayshore Parkway from
San Antonio Road to Garcia Avenue. South of Garcia
Avenue, the sidewalk network on the eastern side of
Bayshore Parkway is incomplete and disconnected for
approximately 800 feet, after which the sidewalk is
narrow (<6 feet), complete, and detached from the street.
Street parking is allowed on the east side of Bayshore
Parkway south of Garcia Avenue. Bayshore Parkway
becomes Salado Drive further to the south, turning
northeast and terminating in a T-intersection with Garcia
Avenue.
Terminal Boulevard has two trailheads for the Bay Trail.
One is at the intersection of Terminal Boulevard and
San Antonio Road, and the other is at the street's
eastern terminus, just past its intersection with
Broderick Way. The south side of Terminal Boulevard
has parallel street parking and fronts older warehouse
& office space. The sidewalk on the south side of
Terminal Boulevard is narrow (approximately 6 feet
wide) and attached to the curb. The northern side of
Terminal Boulevard has perpendicular street parking
and fronts Shoreline Park. A continuous concrete curb
separates the perpendicular parking spaces from the
northern end of the roadway. The concrete curb acts as
a barrier, creating a paved pathway approximately 6.5
feet wide that is overhung by parked car bumpers. This pathway stretches the length of Terminal Boulevard
from one trailhead to the other. The eastern trailhead has a seamless transition from this pathway onto the
park trail. The western trailhead has a vehicle driveway curb cut, a section of unpaved ground, and gauntlet-
style entryway between the pathway and Terminal Boulevard. The ROW for Terminal Boulevard (including
the pathway) is 70 feet.
The streets Broderick Way, Casey Avenue, Marine Way and
Coast Avenue make up the network of roadways in the
northwestern segment of the North Bayshore area. The built
environment is oldest in the northwestern part of the study
area, as other areas have been redeveloped more recently.
The street network, therefore, is also oldest here. All streets
are approximately 45-50 feet in width, allow parallel street
parking, and have no bicycle facilities. All streets have
narrow attached sidewalks (between 5-6 feet in width) on
each side the street. The intersections of each of these
streets have wide curb radii, increasing the crossing distance
and reducing the need for cars to slow as they turn the
corners. Casey Avenue and Coast Avenue are both cul-de-
sacs that provide access to the center of the Intuit campus.

Marine Way travels 0.33 miles between Casey Avenue and Garcia Avenue with no cross streets on its western
side along this stretch, creating a mega-block between Marine Way and Bayshore Parkway.
The bulk of the Intuit campus is situated between
Marine Way in the west, Garcia Avenue in the south,
and Shoreline Park in the east. Intuit is in the midst of a
campus redevelopment, part of which includes the
construction of a new roadway ("Main Street"). This
roadway will travel north/south from Garcia Avenue,
parallel to Marine Way, connecting with the cul-de-
sacs on Casey Avenue and Coast Avenue. The path of
this roadway travels along existing surface parking
lanes in the Intuit campus and will front the main
buildings of the Intuit campus. The new construction
will remove significant surface parking along the
eastern side of "Main Street", replacing it with open
space and a sports court. Parking and building
configurations between "Main Stret" and Marine Way will remain the same.




Garcia Avenue is the primary connecting roadway between
the northwestern (Intuit) section of the study area and the
central (Google) section of the study area. It runs from
Bayshore Parkway in the west to Amphitheatre Parkway in
the east, where it becomes Charleston Road. The right-of-
way is approximately 50 feet wide. Garcia Avenue has bike
lanes, no street parking, and a two-way turn lane that is
converted into a landscaped median in some areas. Garcia
Avenue has complete sidewalks on both sides of the street,
which are detached from the street in large stretches. On the
northern side of the street where Garcia Avenue fronts
Shoreline Park, the detached sidewalk resembles a Class I multi-use path facility, meandering as far as 80 feet
from the roadway.

There is a landscaped median in a short 250 foot stretch of Garcia Avenue, just east of Marine Way, where the
Intuit campus extends to the south of Garcia Avenue. This median has several areas paved with river stones,
suggesting a crossing space for pedestrians. There are pedestrian walkways through the parking lot
landscaping adjacent to Garcia Avenue which line up with these crossing spaces, but there are no marked
crosswalks, curb ramps, or pedestrian signage in the roadway. During the site visit, staff observed a number of
Intuit staffers using these informal crossing spaces when crossing Garcia Avenue. There is 0.3 mile stretch of
road, between Marine Way and Salado Drive, where there are no crosswalks across Garcia Avenue.

Garcia Avenue and Bayshore Parkway create a large mega-block that is built out with office-park style
buildings and extensive surface parking lots.




The specifications of the Rengstorff Avenue Overcrossing have been described inAppendix B. There are
multiple significant conflict points on the bike lane in both directions on the Rengstorff Avenue overcrossing.
Southbound, bicyclists must cross over the on-ramp travel lane (without signage or infrastructure directing
their path) in order to continue. There is a similar merging across the off-ramp travel lane immediately north
of the overcrossing. Northbound, the off-ramp travel lane does not merge with existing lanes, instead
becoming a dedicated right-turn lane at the intersection with Charleston Road. This creates a situation
where bicyclists navigate between two dedicated vehicle travel lanes, where drivers are focused on lane
changes, merging, and are traveling at relatively high speeds.

The single sidewalk on the eastern side of the overcrossing crosses the offramp lane on the northern side of the
overcrossing at a marked crosswalk that has no warning signage for pedestrians. This crosswalk connects to a
detached sidewalk that meanders to the north and to the south through a large landscaped area, but does not
provide a direct path of travel to any of the nearby buildings. The parking lot adjacent to this sidewalk has no
marked route for pedestrians to cross the parking lot or ADA compliant curb ramps.




The specifications of the Permanente Creek Trail were described in Section 3 of the main report. The trail
currently provides no access to the west side of the creek between Highway 101 and Charleston Road. There
is an access road on the top of the western creek levy, with several informal trails through landscaping from
the surface parking lot on the west side of the creek up to the top of the levy. Landings Drive parallels the
west side of the creek. Providing direct access to this roadway from the Permanente Creek Trail could
significantly increase access and trail utility.

From Highway 101 to Charleston Road, a distance of 0.27 miles, there is only one trailhead from the
Permanente Creek Trail to the adjacent Google offices to the east. Creating a well-marked through
connection to Alta Avenue could also improve utility and access for the Permanente Creek Trail.

There is no crossing for the Permanente Creek Trail where
it meets Charleston Road. Where the Permanente Creek
Trail lets out, there is a curb ramp but no crossing signage
or striping. The landscaped median on Charleston Road is
unbroken, with no crossing. Because there is a curbed
median in the street, bicyclists behaving legally can only
travel east on Charleston Road. Many pedestrians and
bicyclists cross the street mid-block in the vicinity of the
trailhead. The City of Mountain View is currently
studying the feasibility of an undercrossing at Charleston
Road and considering improvements to the existing
undercrossing at Amphitheatre Parkway.

North of Charleston Road, the Permanente Creek Trail
crosses under Amphitheatre Parkway and connects to multi-use paths in Shoreline Park on the northern side
of the street. The undercrossing provides trail users with access to both the eastbound and westbound sides
of Amphitheatre Parkway. A Google-built pedestrian bridge crosses Permanente Creek in the block between
Charleston Road and Amphitheatre Parkway, connecting two of the largest complexes in the Google campus
area. Despite this bridge, access to the western levy is restricted from both Charleston Road and
Amphitheatre Parkway. A stairway from the bridge landing provides access to buildings, with a series of
walking paths paralleling the pathway atop the western levy.

The trail currently ends at the southern side of Old Middlefield Way, with an access road continuing south to
Rock Street. The City is currently designing an extension of the trail to Rock Street, and studying the
feasibility of extending the trail from Rock Street to Middlefield Road, where it would connect with the city's
existing bicycle network.




 Alta Avenue, Huff Avenue, Joaquin Road, and Plymouth Street
make up the network of streets encompassing a large portion
of the Google campus, bounded by Highway 101, Permanente
Creek, Charleston Road, and Shoreline Parkway. Each
roadway has a width of 30-32 feet, with two travel lanes, street
parking on some streets, and no bicycle infrastructure. The
sidewalk network is complete in this area, with many of the
sidewalks detached and meandering in elevated landscaping.
All of Alta Avenue, Huff Avenue, and Joaquin Road are 0.25
miles north/south from Charleston Road to Plymouth Street.
There are a number of surface parking lot alleys which travel
east/west through these super-blocks to connect the streets.
There is also a long parallel pedestrian corridor between Huff Avenue and Joaquin Road which almost travels
the length of the block. Many Google shuttles park along these streets when not in use. This area may be
redeveloped by Google following the adoption of the Precise Plan.
Charleston Road connects with Amphitheatre Parkway and Shoreline Parkway, and is the primary corridor of
the Google Campus. Google staff, in previous stakeholder meetings, has entertained the conversion of this
street into a bike/ped/transit priority roadway, acting as a transit hub that does not allow private vehicles.
The VTA Local 40 bus currently runs on Charleston Road.

Although many trails and pedestrian walkways meet
Charleston Road, the only pedestrian crossings are at
intersections with other streets. This forces pedestrians to
either cross mid-block without the aid of markings and/or
signage, or to walk out of their way to a crosswalk at an
intersection. This can dis-incentivize both walking and the
use of designated pedestrian facilities because their utility is
diminished when pedestrians are sent on circuitous routes. If
Charleston Road is to become a transit-priority roadway,
conditions and convenience for pedestrians will need
significant improvements.
Amphitheatre Parkway runs from Charleston Road in the
west to Shoreline Boulevard in the east. It has expansive
sidewalks on both sides of the street and bike lanes. The
northern side of Amphitheatre Parkway fronts Shoreline Park
and there is a sizable park fronting the southern side of the
street to the east of the main Google campus building.
Amphitheatre Parkway has minimal curb cuts and no cross
streets along its length.

A large, curb-separated bus bay is built on Amphitheatre
Parkway for westbound buses immediately east of
Charleston Road. The bus bay was built by Google during
construction of new buildings, and no buses currently use this bus bay.




Microsoft's campus is located in the extreme southeast corner of
the North Bayshore area, bounded by Highway 101, the Stevens
Creek Trail, and La Avenida. La Avenida connects to Shoreline
Boulevard in the west and dead-ends at a trailhead for the
Stevens Creek Trail in the east. The street is approximately 49
feet wide with one travel lane in each direction, street parking,
and no bicycle facilities. The street is designated as a bicycle
route by the City of Mountain View.

Before reaching Shoreline Boulevard, La Avenida necks down to a
one-way street outlet. This section of the roadway is 36 feet
wide, with two westbound travel lanes (expanding to three lanes
at the intersection) and no street parking. The sidewalk on the
southern side of the street is expanded to 17 feet in this section.

A vehicle turnaround and a trailhead for the Stevens Creek Trail are located at the eastern end of La Avenida.
The turnaround is very large, with a diameter or 100 feet. As the primary connector between bike lanes on
Shoreline Boulevard and the Stevens Creek Trail, La Avenida should be prioritized for bike lane
implementation. There are several options for what could be
done at the point where La Avenida necks-down
approaching Shoreline Boulevard: one travel lane could be
removed, with a bike lane and a buffered counter-flow bike
lane installed; the westbound bikelane could cross over to
the southern side of the street and a cycletrack could be
striped along the expanded sidewalk; or the City could direct
bicyclists to detour on the existing bike lanes on Inigo Way
to the Shoreline Boulevard intersection at Pear Avenue.

Inigo Way, with bike lanes and no street parking, is also bare and exposed. The installation of street trees
along this street, or a landscaped median in lieu of the existing two-way center turn lane, could help soften the
current configuration for bicyclists and pedestrians. Pear Avenue is 43 feet wide. If bicyclists are to be routed
through Inigo Way on their way to and from Shoreline Boulevard, ample wayfinding signage should
accompany the route.




The characteristics of the Shoreline Boulevard overcrossing were discussed in Appendix B. The pedestrian
crossings for the on-ramps and off-ramps on the northern side of the overcrossing have recently been re-built,
providing a much safer pedestrian experience with shorter crossing distances. While not as new, the
pedestrian crossings on the southern side of the overcrossing are also relatively well designed.

There are significant conflict points between bicyclists and drivers on this overcrossing. Bike lanes in both
directions must merge, without signage or pavement markings, across onramp lanes for Highway 101. This
merge takes place after the crest of the overcrossing, making bicyclists difficult to see by accelerating drivers.
For the southbound direction, a long stretch of bike lane is of substandard width (3-4 foot), sitting between
the through travel lane and the on-ramp lane. At the overcrossing of the Central Expressway, there are similar
high-speed transitions of the bike lane over on-ramp and off-ramp travel lanes. These types of bicycle facility
are unlikely to entice the "interested but concerned" subset of potential bicyclists.
Shoreline Boulevard south of Charleston Road is a "Major Retail Street" in the 2030 General Plan, meant to act
as a regional retail center with a pedestrian-friendly layout. For this stretch of Shoreline Boulevard, the right-
of-way is approximately 70 feet. There are two travel lanes in each direction, a planted median that gives way
to turn pockets, no street parking, and bike lanes. The turn pockets at each signalized intersection are
outfitted with bicycle loop detectors and stencils. Sidewalks are 5 feet wide and attached to the curb. The
Shoreline Century Theater, on the west side of Shoreline Boulevard immediately north of Highway 101, is set
far back from the street behind plentiful surface parking.

Bike lanes on Shoreline Boulevard end immediately north of
Charleston Road and become a signed bike route. The right-of-
way in this segment is approximately 55 feet. There are two
travel lanes in each direction and a planted median. Sidewalks
are also 5 feet wide in this segment, but are detached from the
curb in a meandering path. The section of Shoreline Boulevard
between Charleston Road and Amphitheatre Parkway presents a
significant gap in the bicycle network. If bike lanes were
implemented on Crittenden Lane, the bike lane gap on Shoreline
Boulevard north of Charleston Road would become even more
apparent.

Curb radii are wide at all intersections on Shoreline Boulevard and crossing distances for pedestrians are long.
The southern end of Shoreline Boulevard has minimal street trees, creating an unpleasant pedestrian
experience on hot days. The countdown interval for pedestrians crossing Shoreline Boulevard is very short,
with slower pedestrians still in the roadway as the signal changes.

All of the bus lines running through the North Bayshore study area travel along Shoreline Boulevard.
Crittenden Lane is in the northeastern corner of the study area,
accessed from Shoreline Boulevard. The eastern end of
Crittenden Lane has a large Google satellite campus. The street
has a planted median, one travel lane in each direction, no street
parking, and no bicycle infrastructure. Each lane is
approximately 20 feet wide. The northern side of the street has
a linear detached sidewalk behind a uniform planter strip with
street trees. The southern side of the street has no sidewalk,
with a planter strip fronting the street in front of a large surface
parking lot. The only building fronting the street on Crittenden
Lane is the firehouse on the corner of Shoreline Boulevard. All
other buildings sit behind large surface parking lots.

The terminus at Crittenden Lane provides one of the two formal trailhead connections from the study area to
the Stevens Creek Trail. Where the graded pathway from Crittenden Lane meets the Stevens Creek Trail, a
bridge continues across Stevens Creek. On the far side of the bridge, the Bay Trail continues to the north, but
access to the south along the levy is restricted to NASA/AMES residents and employees. There is another trail
at the base of the levy on the eastern side of the creek, but it is fenced off from the Stevens Creek Trail. Google
has discussed its plans with the City to build a bridge over Stevens Creek between the NASA Ames campus
and the North Bayshore study area.
The Stevens Creek Trail was discussed in Section 3 of the main
report. There are two informal trail connections between
Crittenden Lane and La Avenida: one from a parking lot adjacent
to the terminus of Charleston Road and one from the parallel
`Fitness Trail' to the north of Charleston Road. Both trail
connections from Charleston Road and the Fitness Trail travel
through a hedge wall, across a PG&E right-of-way, and up an
informal path to the trail on top of the levy. The Charleston Road
connection has a home-made ramp placed against the curb of the
adjacent parking lot so bicyclists don't have to dismount when
crossing the curb. There two informal trail connections from the
Microsoft campus parking lot to the Stevens Creek Trail south of
the trailhead at La Avenida.

Large portions of the Stevens Creek Trail, especially to the south of Highway 101, are part of the Mountain
View parks system and are administered by the Parks Department. Per the City Code, parks and trails close
at sundown. The closure of the Stevens Creek Trail at sundown limits its utility as a transportation corridor,
especially during winter months.




The Moffett Federal Airfield and the NASA/AMES compound
have a series of business and residential development plans. These
plans contain requirements for street sections as well as TDM
goals for transportation mode share. The street sections for the
NASA/AMES redevelopment, as required in the 2002 NRP
development plan, will all have bike lanes and sidewalks. The
NASA/AMES TDM plan calls for a mode share of 12% biking and
10% walking, including the residents of the NASA/AMES area.
Google has concluded an agreement with NASA to develop a satellite campus at NASA/AMES. Google has
discussed with the City of Mountain View its desire to build bridges across Stevens Creek at Crittenden Lane
and Charleston Road, connecting NASA Ames with the North Bayshore study area. The proposed bridge
would only allow High-Occupancy Vehicle traffic, such as shuttle buses. The bridge would also have a
separate bicycle & pedestrian element, connecting to both Crittenden Lane and to the Stevens Creek Trail.




The Moffett Boulevard overcrossing was discussed in Appendix B. The bike lanes on Moffett Boulevard
become shoulders on the overcrossing, but extend both to the north and to the south of the overcrossing. The
bike lanes to the north of the crossing end at the entrance to the Federal Airfield at RT Jones Road. The bike
lanes to the south end at the undercrossing of Highway 85. Moffett Boulevard continues south to the Central
Expressway, where it becomes Castro Street adjacent to the downtown Mountain View transit center.
Moffett Boulevard has two lanes of travel in each direction for its entire length with left turn pockets at
intersections. There are landscaped medians on Moffett Boulevard from Leong Drive to Middlfield Road.
From Middlefield Road to the Central Expressway, the landscaped medians are replaced with a center turn
lane.




The Ellis Street undercrossing was described in Appendix B. The Ellis Street undercrossing has a very limited
right-of-way between support pillars for Highway 101. The Ellis Street bike lanes end at the intersection
immediately south of the undercrossing, providing no dedicated bicycle infrastructure across the freeway.

The City of Mountain View is currently conducting a Pedestrian & Bicyclist Station Area Access study for the
Bayshore NASA LRT station just north of the Ellis Street undercrossing. Recommended improvements could
improve access between the station and the neighborhoods to the south of Highway 101.
The downtown Mountain View transit center is located on Castro Street at the Central Expressway. It has a
Caltrain station, a VTA light rail station, and a bus depot for VTA. Employer shuttles for the North Bayshore
study area pick up from the transit center. None of the VTA buses that stop at the transit center serve the
North Bayshore study area. The station at the downtown Mountain View transit center has capacity for 32
bikes on Caltrain-installed bike racks. There are 118 bike lockers, and a secure bike room with capacity for 52
bikes. The City has installed pennyfarthing-sytle bike racks nearby.

Evelyn Avenue has bike lanes that extended westward only to Hope
Street, just short of the downtown spine on Castro Street. The
Evelyn Avenue bike lanes connect to the Stevens Creek Trail to the
east. The Caltrain Bicycle Access & Parking Plan, published in
2008, recommends the installation of sharrows from Hope to Castro
on Evelyn Avenue and the conversion of the intersection of Evelyn
Avenue at View Street to a 4-way Stop (which has subsequently
been completed). The report also recommends opening a gap in the
parking lot fence at Bush Street to better facilitate bicycle access.

In the northwest corner of the intersection of Castro Street at
Central Expressway is a cul-de-sac terminus of Stierlin Road, a bicycle route connecting to the Central
Avenue bicycle boulevard. Bicyclists often use the wide shoulders of the Central Expressway as a de-facto
bike lane.
Appendix D
Additional Roadways of Interest
Appendix E Additional Roadways of Interest

Collectors and Arterials
Within the North Bayshore study area are groupings of collector and arterial streets that share geographic
proximity and physical characteristics.

Garcia Avenue ­ Charleston Road
Garcia Avenue begins at a T-intersection with Bayshore Parkway in the western area of the North Bayshore
study area. It travels eastward and changes its name to Charleston Road at its intersection with Rengstorff
Avenue. Garcia Avenue has one travel lane in each direction with a center turn lane and Charleston Road has
two lanes of travel in each direction with a landscaped median and left turn pockets. Charleston Road
continues past an intersection with Shoreline Boulevard, reducing to one lane in each direction and
terminating adjacent to the PG&E easement beside the Stevens Creek Trail. Charleston Road has extensive
median landscaping between lanes of travel. There is no street parking allowed on these two streets. Garcia
Avenue ­ Charleston Road have bike lanes and detached sidewalks for pedestrians its entire length.

Alta Avenue ­ Huff Avenue ­ Joaquin Road ­ Plymouth Street
Alta Avenue, Huff Avenue, Joaquin Road, and Plymouth Street are a network of streets bounded by
Permanente Creek on the west, Charleston Road on the north, Shoreline Boulevard on the east, and Highway
101 on the south. These streets serve the heart of the Google campus in the North Shoreline area. They share
the characteristics of having one lane in each direction, no bike lanes, street parking, and detached sidewalks
that are sometimes elevated from the street on landscaped berms.

La Avenida ­ Inigo Way ­ Pear Avenue ­ Space Park Way
La Avenida, Inigo Way, and Pear Avenue make a couplet for access to and from Shoreline Boulevard in the
southeastern corner of the North Bayshore study area. Space Park Way is a cul-de-sac to the north of, and
parallel to, Pear Avenue. Each street has one lane of travel in each direction and attached sidewalks. Inigo
Way also has a center turn lane and bike lanes. Inigo Way does not allow on-street parking, but all other
streets do. La Avenida has a neckdown in advance of its intersection with Shoreline Boulevard, only allowing
westbound traffic out onto Shoreline Boulevard and not allowing traffic from Shoreline Boulevard to turn onto
La Avenida. Drivers are routed around through Pear Avenue and Inigo Way to access La Avenida.

Shorebird Way ­ Charleston Road ­ Stierlin Court ­ Crittenden Lane
Shorebird Way, Charleston Road, Stierlin Court, and Crittenden Lane are a parallel grouping of streets in the
northeastern corner of the North Bayshore study area. All of the streets have one lane of travel in each
direction and none of the streets allow on-street parking. Charleston Road has bike lanes and both Stierlin
Court and Crittenden lane have landscaped medians between directions of travel. Stierlin Road is a private
street, and thus has non-conforming street designs, such as crosswalks marked in the driveways to adjacent
parking lots. Shorebird Way turns north and ends in a T-intersection at Charleston Road. All other streets
dead-end before reaching the Stevens Creek Trail. Shorebird Way, Charleston Road, and Crittenden Lane all
have sidewalks on only one side of the street; Stierlin Court has no sidewalks.
Marine Way ­ Coast Avenue ­ Casey Avenue ­ Broderick Way ­ Terminal
Boulevard
Marine Way, Coast Avenue, Casey Avenue, Broderick Way, and Terminal Boulevard are all located in the
northwestern corner of the study area. These streets have an industrial layout, with wide curb radii at
intersections and narrow sidewalks. There is one lane of travel in either direction for all streets, and none of
the streets have bike lanes. Street parking is allowed on all of these streets.

Freeway Crossings
San Antonio Road at Highway 101
The northwestern corner of the Bayshore area is accessed via the San Antonio Road overcrossing of Highway
101. This overcrossing has one lanes of vehicle travel in either directions with no bicycle facilities and a
sidewalk on the northwestern side of the overcrossing. The vehicle right-of-way on the overpass is
approximately 27 feet. The single sidewalk, on the northwestern side of the overcrossing, is approximately 6
feet wide; this sidewalk crosses a freeway onramp to the north of Highway 101 and an offramp to the south.

A marked crosswalk exists on at the northern onramp, which connects to the sidewalk network of the North
Bayshore area. At the southern offramp, a curb ramp is built into the sidewalk, but there is no marked
crosswalk. Furthermore, there is no sidewalk on the far side of the offramp travel lane. Sidewalk begins
approximately 20 feet to the south of this unmarked crossing. Southbound pedestrians must travel .36 miles
from this crossing before reaching the first intersection south of the overpass. There is a large shopping center
along the western side of San Antonio Road, but no access for bicyclists or pedestrians between the roadway
and the shopping center.

The City of Mountain View controls San Antonio Road north of the Highway 101 overpass, while the City of
Palo Alto controls San Antonio Road south to the Central Expressway.

Rengstorff Avenue at Highway 101
Rengstorff Avenue crosses over Highway 101 near the center of the North Bayshore study area. The
overcrossing has two parallel roadways over the freeway, one for each direction of travel. There are two travel
lanes in each direction, bike lanes in both directions, and a sidewalk on southeastern side of the overpass.
Both roadways on this overcrossing have a right-of-way of approximately 27 feet, shared between two travel
lanes and a bike lane. The sidewalk on the southeastern overcrossing roadway is approximately 6 feet wide.

The sidewalk has a narrow terminus north of Highway 101 where it is cut off by the off-ramp travel lane. A
marked crosswalk at this location connects to detached sidewalk. At the southern end of the overcrossing,
the sidewalk travels through a pedestrian island when crossing an on-ramp and off-ramp.

The southbound bike lane on Rengstorff Avenue must merge across onramp and offramp traffic to the north of
Highway 101. The northbound bike lane on Rengstorff Avenue, to the north of Highway 101, must travel to the
left side of the offramp lane of travel for approximately 600 feet until a signalized intersection at Charleston
Road. The Rengstorff Avenue bike lanes extend south to the Mountain View city limits at El Camino Real.
The bike lanes have on-street parking for most of their length to the south of Highway 101.
Shoreline Boulevard at Highway 101
The Shoreline Boulevard overcrossing provides access to the eastern part of the study area. The overcrossing
has two through travel lanes in each direction, one onramp/offramp lane in each direction that stretches the
length of the overcrossing, bike lanes in both directions, and sidewalks on both sides of the overcrossing. A
large planted median strip divides the two directions of travel. The northbound right-of-way is
approximately 40 feet wide and the southbound right-of-way is approximately 36 feet wide. The sidewalks
on each side of the overcrossing are both approximately 8 feet wide.

The crosswalks for the on-ramps and off-ramps on the northern side of the overcrossing have recently been re-
built, providing shorter crossing distances and enhanced crossing amenities.

The bike lanes in both directions must transition from a curb-adjacent position on the overcrossing to the left
side of the highway onramp lane. This transition occurs immediately after the crest of the overcrossing. For
the southbound direction, the bike lane is of a substandard (3-4 feet) width between the through travel lane
and the on-ramp lane.

The bike lanes on Shoreline Boulevard extend south to Mountain View city limits. The bike lanes have no on-
street parking for most of their length. At the overcrossing of the Central Expressway, there are similar
transitions of the bike lane across onramp travel lanes.

Moffett Boulevard at Highway 101
The Moffett Boulevard overcrossing provides access between downtown Mountain View and the Moffett Air
Base. This overcrossing is immediately east of the study area. Moffett Boulevard has two lanes of travel in
each direction, bike lanes in both directions, and a sidewalk on the eastern side of the street. The vehicle
right-of-way is approximately 55 feet. The sidewalk is approximately 6 feet wide.

The bike lanes on the overcrossing are of substandard width and are not signed as bike lanes, despite the
presence of bike lanes to the north and to the south of the overcrossing on Moffett Boulevard. Northbound
bicyclists must merge across an onramp pocket to the north of the overcrossing. The bike lanes extend south
to Highway 85, where they connect with the Stevens Creek Trail.

Ellis Street at Highway 101
East of Moffett Boulevard is an undercrossing of Highway 101 at Ellis Street, also the location of a VTA light
rail crossing. The right-of-way is constrained by support pillars for the freeway being built in the median of
the roadway, with two travel lanes in each direction. The southbound right-of-way is approximately 27 feet
wide and the northbound right-of-way is approximately 32 feet wide. To the south of this crossing, Ellis
Street has bike lanes in both directions to Middlefield Road. There is sidewalk on only the western side of the
undercrossing and is 5 feet wide. On the east side of the undercrossing are the VTA light rail tracks.

SHORELINE TRANSPORTATION STUDY                                                                                                                           ii
Technical Report
Transit Technologies
Introduction
This report documents the work that was performed to define and evaluate the various alternative
transit improvements that were developed as part of the Shoreline Transportation Study. It is
important to note that all of the work presented here represents very preliminary planning and
conceptualization efforts and that significant further study and design work would be needed before
moving forward with any of the identified improvements. The alternatives identified here were
developed with the intent to enhance and reinforce the existing network of regional and local transit
services. The major transit improvements being pursued by the regional transit operators offer an
opportunity to increase the accessibility of the North Bayshore by improving the local connectivity
between North Bayshore and the nearby regional transit hubs such as the Mountain View Transit
Center in the downtown. These regional improvements include:

       Caltrain Modernization and Electrification ­ Caltrain is in the process of planning and
       implementing system electrification and train control enhancements which will allow increased
       service efficiency, reliability and capacity.

       VTA Light Rail Enhancements ­ The VTA is planning enhancement to the light rail system that
       will include double tracking of the service to Mountain View, and express train services. These
       improvements will increase the attractiveness of the service to Mountain View and will increase
       the capacity of the system significantly.

       BART Silicon Valley Extension ­ The southward extension of BART in the East Bay will provide
       a new transit opportunity for those traveling from the east and southeast to access North
       Bayshore via the improved VTA light rail system or via express bus services.

       BRT and Regional Express Bus Services ­ Bus Rapid Transit services are being planned by both
       VTA and Samtrans along the El Camino Real corridor. In addition, the Dumbarton Express Bus
       service is considering a possible new route serving South Bay destinations such as North
       Bayshore.

One of the major transportation challenges for the North Bayshore will be how to provide the
infrastructure and the transit services necessary to take full advantage of the regional transit
improvements identified above. Today a variety of shuttle bus services and some limited local bus
services are the primary linkage to the regional transit network. Bicycles also serve a vital role in
moving transit riders between North Bayshore and the regional transit hub in downtown Mountain
View. Transit vehicles now must compete with the intense auto traffic which saturates many of the
routes leading into the North Bayshore.

Modal Considerations
A variety of transit modes and transit operational concepts are available to provide improved transit
connections and access to North Bayshore. These modal options are summarized in Table 1.

Evolution of Transit System Capacity
An important concept of transit system development is the idea of phased implementation with the
goal of providing a logical transition from a low capacity bus based system operating on city streets to
a higher capacity system with its own dedicated right-of-way. Two possible approaches to provide this
type of system evolution are outlined here.

Case 1
This example involves a three step process to transition from bus to a higher capacity and higher
service quality system. The three steps are as follows and as illustrated in Figure 1:

    1. Transit-only lanes for shuttles and buses with some shared ROW ­ this begins the process of
       securing ROW for both near- and long- term transit solutions

    2. As growth in the area continues, the capacity of the transit-only lanes can be increased by
       transition to some semi-exclusive ROW lanes that bypass traffic-heavy areas in a phased
       manner. These lanes can also be made available to HOV or autonomous vehicles to increase
       throughput.

    3. In the long term, there is potential for semi-exclusive ROW lanes to be transitioned into fully
       grade-separated infrastructure for a higher capacity transit system--such as an APM, PRT, or
       LRT system.



Figure 1: Case 1 ­ Three Step Technology Transition




SHORELINE TRANSPORTATION STUDY                                                                   3
Technical Report · Transit Technologies



Case 2
The second example involves a simpler two step transition process. The steps are outlined below and
shown in Figure 2.

    1.    Transit-only lanes for shuttles and buses with some shared ROW ­ this begins the process of
         securing ROW for both near- and long-term transit solutions.

    2. As growth in the area continues, the capacity of the transit-only lanes can be increased by
       transition to an at-grade LRT or streetcar system. This would reduce the amount of elevated
       structures and guideway needed and could eliminate or reduce the need for separated grade
       crossings.

Figure 2: Case 2 ­ Two Step Technology Transition




Future Transit Demands
A key determinant in the selection of the appropriate transit technology to provide the linkage
between the Mountain View Transit Center and the North Bayshore is the potential magnitude of the
transit demand to be served. Table 2 provides representative information on the demand levels
expected from both Caltrain and VTA light rail at the Mountain View Transit Center under existing
conditions and for several potential future development scenarios. When sizing a transit system to
potential demand levels it is important to consider that the system must be capable of accommodating
the peak surges of demand that will occur. This is represented by the application of a 1.5 peak surge
factor to the 15 minute peak volume of passengers as shown in the far right column of the table.
Currently the peak 15 minute surge demand is estimated to be about 100 passengers in a 15 minute
period. Under the General Plan ­ 2030 scenario this would increase to about 535 passengers, or more
than five times the existing level.




SHORELINE TRANSPORTATION STUDY                                                             4
                                                                      Technical Report · Transit Technologies

                                   Caltrain      Peak 3 Hr.                        15-minute
                                 Demand to      LRT Demand                        demand plus
                                    North         to North       Total 3 Hr.       1.5 "peak
           Scenario               Bayshore        Bayshore        Demand            factor"
 Existing                                 700            100              800              100
 General Plan - 2030                    2,680          1,600            4,280              535
 General Plan - High-Growth             6,000          3,500            9,500            1,190


Table 3 shows how these peak surge demand factors can be translated into the number of transit
vehicles required to serve the demand for each transit technology type. For example with a shuttle
bus system it would take five buses to serve the existing 15 minute peak surge demand of 100
passengers. The number of shuttle buses required would increase dramatically under the GP-2030
and GP-High Growth scenarios, with 27 buses required for the GP-2030 scenario and 60 vehicles
required under the GP-High Growth Scenario. Similar information is provided for each of the
candidate technologies. PRT would require the greatest number of vehicles because PRT vehicles
intentionally have a small capacity in order to provide the personalized door-to-door service that is
the trademark of PRT. Autonomous Vehicles would have a similar characteristic, although it is
possible to have autonomous buses which then would be similar in capacity to the shuttle bus or
conventional bus technologies. GRT (Group Rapid Transit) represents a compromise between PRT
and the higher capacity systems such as LRT and APM. An example of an APM system would be a
system which would share a single dual-lane guideway between the downtown transit center and
North Bayshore and then would have multiple branches each serving a specific area of North
Bayshore. The vehicles leaving the transit center in downtown would be branded or dedicated to
serve a specific branch line destination in North Bayshore.

For example, a shuttle bus operation would require 27 vehicles to serve the demand. Based on the
length of the trip to North Bayshore and the running time estimated for each bus trip the frequency of
the service was calculated. As indicated in the table a shuttle bus would have to arrive at the Mountain
View Transit Center every 0.6 minutes or every 36 seconds in order to provide the needed capacity.
Similarly PRT vehicles would have to arrive at the transit center every 0.1 minutes or every 6 seconds
to meet the demand.

Operational Considerations
In terms of the linkage between the Mountain View Transit Center and North Bayshore there are three
important considerations regarding the ability of a given transit technology to provide an efficient
connection:

    1. There needs to be adequate space for the staging and loading/unloading of the transit vehicles
       at the Mountain View Transit Center.

    2. The linkage between the Transit Center and North Bayshore needs to provide sufficient
       capacity and reliability to accommodate the required flow of transit vehicles during the peak
       periods.

    3. There needs to be a suitable system for distributing passengers once they arrive in North
       Bayshore. Ideally this would not require a change of transit mode.

Transit Center Capacity
The current Mountain View Transit Center already experiences capacity issues during peak times.
The shuttle bus operations cannot be accommodated in the existing bus loading area and they must
load and unload on the adjacent street frontages. The significant expansion of shuttle operations
required to address the growth of the North Bayshore will result in significant operational problems
with the current transit center configuration. An expanded shuttle bus facility is needed. Over time,
however, even with an expanded facility it will become challenging to accommodate all the buses. For
example, under the General Plan ­ 2030 scenario a shuttle bus would arrive every 36 seconds. As the



SHORELINE TRANSPORTATION STUDY                                                               6
                                                                       Technical Report · Transit Technologies



time required for unloading and loading passengers will be longer than 36 seconds there will need to
be space to accommodate the buses as they accumulate. Ideally the buses should be waiting for the
train to arrive so that passengers would not have to wait. Similarly they should arrive just before a
train departs. This will require a large bus loading area. The technologies with lower per vehicle
passenger capacities will face similar challenges. This is particularly true with PRT where a vehicle
would be arriving at the transit center every 6 seconds. The station facility for the PRT will have to be
quite large and may prove difficult to accommodate. Most likely the PRT system would require an
elevated station area located on top of the existing transit center.

Connection to North Bayshore
The shuttle buses currently must circulate through the downtown area in order to reach the viable
routes to North Bayshore. A variety of routes are used depending on traffic conditions. As the number
of shuttle buses increases it will become increasingly difficult to provide a reliable service. Already
today employees complain about the large amount of time required to make the short trip to the
transit center. Moving the shuttle bus boarding area to the north side of the transit center would help
greatly. Priority treatments for transit vehicles on the routes to North Bayshore such as Shoreline
Boulevard would also help. In order to efficiently accommodate the amount of transit riders that are
expected, it will eventually be necessary to provide a route that is fully exclusive or at least exclusive
from general traffic through the congested area. The Shoreline Transit Bridge concept is a good
example of how this could be accomplished. This would be a separate bridge for transit vehicles
parallel to Shoreline Boulevard across U.S. Highway 101. It will be important, however, to make sure
that a right-of-way is preserved that would be capable of accommodating one or more of the higher
capacity transit systems in the future when shuttle buses will not be able to effectively accommodate
the demand.

Distribution in North Bayshore
North Bayshore is a large enough area to make the distribution of employees using a transit
connection from the Mountain View Transit Center a complex issue. Ideally employees would like to
be dropped off close to their workplace. Currently this either requires multiple stops or multiple
shuttle routes that specifically serve a given portion of North Bayshore. One improvement that could
be made to the existing shuttle service would be to consolidate the multiple services provided by the
individual employers into two or more zone based shuttle routes. The routes could be color coded by
the zone or area to be service, for example, the red route could serve all the employers in one zone,
and the blue route could serve all the employers in a second zone, and so forth.

PRT technology is specifically designed to address this issue of multiple dispersed destinations, as it
offers the potential of direct origin station to destination station service by means of a guideway
network. The autonomous vehicle technology further improves on this by providing the possibility for
door-to-door service, as it is not tied to a guideway. Similar to PRT technology, GRT technology
provides direct station to station service, but may not consist of the same extensive network and
number of stations. Instead, GRT technology may be developed in a simplified network or branching
configuration, involving multiple routes, each of which would serve specific portions of North
Bayshore. While branching can be done with the other higher capacity technologies such as LRT and
APM, it tends to result in a need to operate more capacity than is needed if the desired frequency of
service is provided on all branches.




SHORELINE TRANSPORTATION STUDY                                                                   7
Technical Report · Transit Technologies

Order of magnitude estimates of costs have been prepared for each of the transit technologies. These
are based on very conceptual definitions of the transit improvements which were developed without
any engineering design efforts. As such they should be used with caution. The costs are based upon
systems designed to serve the level of transit use associated with the General Plan ­ 2030 growth
scenario. All costs are in 2012 dollars.

Personal Rapid Transit (PRT)
The concept for PRT was defined in terms of the same two components used to define the APM system
­ the connection to North Bayshore and then a PRT system internal to North Bayshore. As with the
APM system, a dual lane connection is assumed between the Caltrain station and North Bayshore. The
PRT system internal to North Bay is assumed as an elevated guideway serving several nodes
throughout the Shoreline Area. The all inclusive lump sum cost assumed a distributed network of 8-
10 miles of single lane guideway. The unit cost is taken from the Viability of Personal Rapid Transit In
New Jersey, Final Report, 2007, developed for the New Jersey Department of Transportation Bureau of
Research. The accuracy of the PRT cost estimate has many uncertainties and significant risk due to
the very limited amount of actual PRT construction experience that is available at this point. The PRT
system could be implemented through a phased construction program as demand warrants.

Table 15 provides the capital cost estimates for the connection to Caltrain and Table 16 provides the
cost for the internal distribution system in North Bayshore. The two components combined would
have a capital cost totaling $1.2 billion.

Autonomous On-Demand Vehicle System
This concept involves a system of autonomous vehicles that would be made available to North
Bayshore employees to make the connection to the Mountain View Transit Center. The vehicles would
operate on existing streets, although potentially they could take advantage of the special transit only
facilities and bridges that were considered for the shuttle bus and LRT options. There is no reliable
source of cost data for these vehicles, which are still in the experimental testing phase of development.
The cost estimate assumes that the costs would be similar to those of a conventional vehicle plus an
added 33% to cover the costs of the autonomous control systems.

                                                                     Technical Report · Transit Technologies

Findings
Any of the identified transit technologies could be used to provide connectivity between the Mountain
View Transportation Center and North Bayshore. The following are some conclusions resulting from
the technology review:

       A shared shuttle system would be an effective near-term to mid-term approach to provide the
       transit connection. The system involves a relatively small capital costs. However, as North
       Bayshore employment grows the sheer number of shuttle buses required will create the need to
       develop a new shuttle facility at the Mountain View Transit Center. Also the cost of operations
       and maintenance will grow significantly. At some point as employment approaches the
       General Plan ­ 2030 level ­ it will be more cost effective to move to a higher capacity, more
       reliable transit technology.

       Using a PRT system to make the connection will pose challenges in terms of the configuration of
       the PRT terminal at the Mountain View Transit Center. The large number of vehicles required
       will be difficult to accommodate. The layout of the area for passenger boarding and alighting
       will also be challenging. The PRT technology is well suited to provide good distribution and
       circulation within North Bayshore.

       A GRT technology may address some of the logistical challenges of the PRT technology in terms
       of the terminal at the transit center. This technology would also be well suited to provide good
       distribution and circulation within North Bayshore. GRT system costs would be similar to APM
       costs on a per mile basis.

       It will be critical to identify the route of the proposed future transit connection and to take the
       steps to establish and preserve the right-of-way required consistent with the 3 step or 2 step
       transition concepts outlined earlier in this report.
